	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Yorkshire and the Humber</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the region. For the European constituency, see <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_and_the_Humber_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="Yorkshire and the Humber (European Parliament constituency)">Yorkshire and the Humber (European Parliament constituency)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A region of England</div>

<p><b>Yorkshire and the Humber</b> is one of nine official <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">regions of England</a> at the <a href="/wiki/First-level_NUTS_of_the_European_Union" title="First-level NUTS of the European Union">first level</a> of <a href="/wiki/NUTS_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="NUTS of the United Kingdom">Nomenclature of Territorial Units for Statistics</a> (NUTS) for <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">statistical</a> purposes. It comprises most of <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a> (the administrative areas of <a href="/wiki/South_Yorkshire" title="South Yorkshire">South Yorkshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Riding_of_Yorkshire" title="East Riding of Yorkshire">East Riding of Yorkshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Hull</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire" title="North Yorkshire">North Yorkshire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/City_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="City of York">City of York</a>), as well as <a href="/wiki/North_Lincolnshire" title="North Lincolnshire">North Lincolnshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_East_Lincolnshire" title="North East Lincolnshire">North East Lincolnshire</a>. It does not include <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough" title="Middlesbrough">Middlesbrough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Redcar_and_Cleveland" title="Redcar and Cleveland">Redcar and Cleveland</a> or other areas of the <a href="/wiki/Historic_counties_of_England" title="Historic counties of England">historic county</a> of Yorkshire, such as <a href="/wiki/Sedbergh" title="Sedbergh">Sedbergh</a> not included in the aforementioned administrative areas. The largest settlements are, <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bradford" title="Bradford">Bradford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Hull</a>, and <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>. The population in 2011 was 5,284,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Region_populations_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Region_populations-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The committees for the regions, including the one for Yorkshire and the Humber, ceased to exist upon the dissolution of Parliament on 12 April 2010; they were not re-established by the newly elected House.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Regional ministers were not reappointed by the incoming <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron–Clegg coalition">Coalition Government</a>, and the Government Offices were abolished in 2011.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_context">Geographical context</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geographical context">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox vcard" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;width:21.0em;"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><div class="adr" style="font-size:smaller"><span class="fn org country-name"><a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a></span>

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Topographical_areas_of_Yorkshire" title="Topographical areas of Yorkshire">Topographical areas of Yorkshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Yorkshire" title="Geology of Yorkshire">Geology of Yorkshire</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Scammonden_Reservoir" title="Scammonden Reservoir">Scammonden Dam</a>, is the highest dam in UK at 73 metres, and Dean Head cutting is the deepest roadway <a href="/wiki/Cut_(earthmoving)" title="Cut (earthmoving)">cutting</a> in Europe at 183&#160;ft, at <a href="/wiki/Scammonden_Bridge" title="Scammonden Bridge">Scammonden Bridge</a>, on the M62. <a href="/wiki/Sutton-under-Whitestonecliffe" title="Sutton-under-Whitestonecliffe">Sutton-under-Whitestonecliffe</a> claims to be longest place name in England.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the Yorkshire and the Humber region, there is a very close relationship between the major <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topographical</a> areas and the underlying geology.<sup id="cite_ref-genukiorg_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-genukiorg-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pennines" title="Pennines">Pennine</a> chain of hills in the west is of <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> origin. The central vale is <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Permo-Triassic</a>. The <a href="/wiki/North_York_Moors" title="North York Moors">North York Moors</a> in the north-east of the county are <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> in age, while the <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_Wolds" title="Yorkshire Wolds">Yorkshire Wolds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_Wolds" title="Lincolnshire Wolds">Lincolnshire Wolds</a> to the south east are <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> chalk uplands.<sup id="cite_ref-genukiorg_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-genukiorg-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The highest point of the region is <a href="/wiki/Whernside" title="Whernside">Whernside</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_Dales" title="Yorkshire Dales">Yorkshire Dales</a>, at 737 metres (2,418&#160;ft).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivers">Rivers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rivers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yorkshire-Drainage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Yorkshire-Drainage.jpg/170px-Yorkshire-Drainage.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Yorkshire-Drainage.jpg/255px-Yorkshire-Drainage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Yorkshire-Drainage.jpg/340px-Yorkshire-Drainage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="738" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yorkshire-Drainage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main rivers of Yorkshire.</div></div></div>
<p>The region is <a href="/wiki/Drainage" title="Drainage">drained</a> by several rivers. In western and central Yorkshire, the many rivers empty their waters into the <a href="/wiki/River_Ouse,_Yorkshire" title="River Ouse, Yorkshire">River Ouse</a>, which reaches the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Humber" title="Humber">Humber Estuary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rivers_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rivers-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>  The most northerly of the rivers in the Ouse system is the <a href="/wiki/River_Swale" title="River Swale">River Swale</a>, which drains <a href="/wiki/Swaledale" title="Swaledale">Swaledale</a> before passing through <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_North_Yorkshire" title="Richmond, North Yorkshire">Richmond</a> and meandering across the <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_Mowbray" title="Vale of Mowbray">Vale of Mowbray</a>. Next, draining <a href="/wiki/Wensleydale" title="Wensleydale">Wensleydale</a>, is the <a href="/wiki/River_Ure" title="River Ure">River Ure</a>, which joins the Swale east of Boroughbridge. The <a href="/wiki/River_Nidd" title="River Nidd">River Nidd</a> rises on the edge of the <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_Dales" title="Yorkshire Dales">Yorkshire Dales National Park</a> and flows along <a href="/wiki/Nidderdale" title="Nidderdale">Nidderdale</a> before reaching the <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_York" title="Vale of York">Vale of York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rivers_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rivers-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
The Ouse is the name given to the river after its confluence with the Ure at Ouse Gill Beck. The <a href="/wiki/River_Wharfe" title="River Wharfe">River Wharfe</a>, which drains <a href="/wiki/Wharfedale" title="Wharfedale">Wharfedale</a>, joins the Ouse upstream of <a href="/wiki/Cawood" title="Cawood">Cawood</a>. The Rivers <a href="/wiki/River_Aire" title="River Aire">Aire</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Calder,_West_Yorkshire" title="River Calder, West Yorkshire">Calder</a> are more southerly contributors to the River Ouse. The most southerly Yorkshire tributary is the <a href="/wiki/River_Don,_South_Yorkshire" class="mw-redirect" title="River Don, South Yorkshire">River Don</a>, which flows northwards to join the main river at <a href="/wiki/Goole" title="Goole">Goole</a>.
The <a href="/wiki/River_Derwent,_Yorkshire" title="River Derwent, Yorkshire">River Derwent</a> rises on the North York Moors, flows south then westwards through the <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_Pickering" title="Vale of Pickering">Vale of Pickering</a>, then turns south again to drain the eastern part of the Vale of York. It empties into the River Ouse at <a href="/wiki/Barmby_on_the_Marsh" title="Barmby on the Marsh">Barmby on the Marsh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rivers_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rivers-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the far north of the county, the <a href="/wiki/River_Tees" title="River Tees">River Tees</a> flows eastwards through <a href="/wiki/Teesdale" title="Teesdale">Teesdale</a> and empties its waters into the North Sea downstream of <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough" title="Middlesbrough">Middlesbrough</a>. The smaller <a href="/wiki/River_Esk,_North_Yorkshire" title="River Esk, North Yorkshire">River Esk</a> flows from west to east at the northern foot of the North York Moors to reach the sea at <a href="/wiki/Whitby" title="Whitby">Whitby</a>.
To the east of the Yorkshire Wolds, the <a href="/wiki/River_Hull" title="River Hull">River Hull</a> flows southwards to join the Humber Estuary at <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Kingston upon Hull</a>. The western Pennines are served by the <a href="/wiki/River_Ribble" title="River Ribble">River Ribble</a>, which drains westwards into the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a> close to <a href="/wiki/Lytham_St_Annes" title="Lytham St Annes">Lytham St Annes</a>.
The lower stretches of the <a href="/wiki/River_Trent" title="River Trent">River Trent</a> flow through North Lincolnshire and meet the Ouse at <a href="/wiki/Trent_Falls" title="Trent Falls">Trent Falls</a>; the <a href="/wiki/River_Ancholme" title="River Ancholme">River Ancholme</a> drains into the Humber Estuary at <a href="/wiki/South_Ferriby" title="South Ferriby">South Ferriby</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rivers_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rivers-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Humber_Bridge_South_Bank2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Humber_Bridge_South_Bank2.jpg/220px-Humber_Bridge_South_Bank2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Humber_Bridge_South_Bank2.jpg/330px-Humber_Bridge_South_Bank2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Humber_Bridge_South_Bank2.jpg/440px-Humber_Bridge_South_Bank2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="783" data-file-height="588" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Humber_Bridge_South_Bank2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Humber_Bridge" title="Humber Bridge">Humber Bridge</a> was designed based on ideas by Sir <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Freeman_(1911%E2%80%931998)" title="Ralph Freeman (1911–1998)">Ralph Freeman</a> before the 1950s, then Sir <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Roberts" title="Gilbert Roberts">Gilbert Roberts</a> in 1955 and 1964, and a final complete design by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bernard_Wex&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bernard Wex (page does not exist)">Bernard Wex</a>. It was made with a significant amount of <a href="/wiki/Ground_granulated_blast-furnace_slag" title="Ground granulated blast-furnace slag">ground granulated blast-furnace slag</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The largest freshwater lake in the region is <a href="/wiki/Hornsea_Mere" title="Hornsea Mere">Hornsea Mere</a> in the <a href="/wiki/East_Riding_of_Yorkshire" title="East Riding of Yorkshire">East Riding of Yorkshire</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This region of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> generally has cool summers and relatively mild winters, with the upland areas of the <a href="/wiki/North_York_Moors" title="North York Moors">North York Moors</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pennines" title="Pennines">Pennines</a> experiencing the coolest weather and the <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_York" title="Vale of York">Vale of York</a> the warmest. Weather conditions vary from day to day as well as from season to season. The latitude of the area means that it is influenced by predominantly westerly winds with depressions and their associated fronts, bringing with them unsettled and windy weather, particularly in winter. Between depressions, there are often small mobile anticyclones that bring periods of fair weather. In winter <a href="/wiki/Anticyclone" title="Anticyclone">anticyclones</a> bring cold dry weather. In summer the anticyclones tend to bring dry, settled conditions which can lead to drought. For its latitude, this area is mild in winter and cooler in summer due to the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> in the northern <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>.
</p><p>Air temperature varies on a daily and seasonal basis. Cities such as Sheffield, Leeds, and Bradford are generally cooler due to their inland and upland location, while York, Hull, and Wakefield are warmer due to their lowland location. The temperature is usually lower at night; January is the coldest time of the year and July is usually the warmest month.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Snow is not uncommon in the winter, Yorkshire is mostly hilly/mountainous, and the Yorkshire Dales and the Pennines can have extreme snowstorms with high snowdrifts. Inland/upland settlements, such as Skipton or Ilkley, have more snow than coastal towns. Hull and Scarborough have less snow as their weather is moderated by the ocean.
</p><p>Climate data for settlements in the region:

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Leeds" title="Architecture of Leeds">Architecture of Leeds</a></div>
<p>There are seven <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">cities</a> in Yorkshire and the Humber: <a href="/wiki/Bradford" title="Bradford">Bradford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Kingston upon Hull</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ripon" title="Ripon">Ripon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wakefield" title="Wakefield">Wakefield</a> and <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>.  Large towns in the area include <a href="/wiki/Barnsley" title="Barnsley">Barnsley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doncaster" title="Doncaster">Doncaster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grimsby" title="Grimsby">Grimsby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halifax,_West_Yorkshire" title="Halifax, West Yorkshire">Halifax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harrogate" title="Harrogate">Harrogate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield" title="Huddersfield">Huddersfield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scunthorpe" title="Scunthorpe">Scunthorpe</a>. Leeds is the largest settlement and the largest part of an urban area with a population of 1.5&#160;million.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2018)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>   Leeds is now<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2018)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> one of the largest financial centres in the United Kingdom.  Sheffield is the second-largest settlement and is a large manufacturing centre.  Bradford is the third-largest city and was a textile manufacturing city. As jobs moved offshore the decline of this industry has resulted in a more diverse economy. Kingston upon Hull is the main port in the region and historically a notable fishing harbour.  While cities such as Bradford, Leeds and Sheffield grew during the industrial revolution, older cities in the region, such as Wakefield and York, were established in trade and politics before them.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_Yorkshire" title="List of tallest buildings in Yorkshire">tallest occupied building in Yorkshire</a> is <a href="/wiki/Bridgewater_Place" title="Bridgewater Place">Bridgewater Place</a> in central Leeds, commonly referred to as <i>The <a href="/wiki/Dalek" title="Dalek">Dalek</a>.</i> Built in 2007, at 361&#160;feet it is the 34th tallest building in the UK. The 37-storey <a href="/wiki/Sky_Plaza" title="Sky Plaza">Sky Plaza</a> in Leeds is the world's second-tallest student accommodation building at 338&#160;feet, and the second tallest building in the region, and the UK's 42nd tallest building, built in 2009. The 32-storey <a href="/wiki/St_Pauls_Tower" class="mw-redirect" title="St Pauls Tower">St Pauls Tower</a> is now the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_and_structures_in_Sheffield" title="List of tallest buildings and structures in Sheffield">tallest building in Sheffield</a> at 331&#160;feet. The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hallamshire_Hospital" title="Royal Hallamshire Hospital">Royal Hallamshire Hospital</a> at 249&#160;feet is the tallest hospital in the UK outside of London, and was built in 1979.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</p>

<p>Key: †<a href="/wiki/Shire_county" class="mw-redirect" title="Shire county">shire county</a> | *<a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_county" title="Metropolitan county">metropolitan county</a>
</p><p>North Lincolnshire, North East Lincolnshire, East Riding of Yorkshire and Kingston upon Hull unitary authorities used to form the non-metropolitan county of Humberside which meant the region was called Yorkshire &amp; Humberside.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Star_Carr" title="Star Carr">Star Carr</a>, south of Scarborough, is the earliest known dwelling in the UK – 8700&#160;BC, in the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> age.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Towton" title="Battle of Towton">Battle of Towton</a> in March 1461, in the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>, is the most costly on British soil. <a href="/wiki/William_Bradley_(giant)" title="William Bradley (giant)">William Bradley</a> of <a href="/wiki/Market_Weighton" title="Market Weighton">Market Weighton</a> is the tallest British man ever, being 7&#160;ft 9&#160;in, known as the <i>Yorkshire Giant</i>. Bradford had the first <a href="/wiki/Trolleybus" title="Trolleybus">trolley bus</a> in the UK in 1910.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wentworth_Woodhouse" title="Wentworth Woodhouse">Wentworth Woodhouse</a> is the largest private house in the UK, off the B6090, which housed until 1979 the <a href="/wiki/Lady_Mabel_College_of_Physical_Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady Mabel College of Physical Education">Lady Mabel College of Physical Education</a>, for female PE teachers, later owned by <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_City_Polytechnic" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheffield City Polytechnic">Sheffield City Polytechnic</a> until 1988, north of Rotherham and east of M1 junction 35a.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Short_Tucano" title="Short Tucano">Tucano</a> entered service with the RAF with <a href="/wiki/No._7_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 7 Flying Training School RAF">7 FTS</a> in December 1989 at RAF Church Fenton.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> On 10 May 1990 at RAF Linton-on-Ouse, <a href="/wiki/Flight_lieutenant" title="Flight lieutenant">Flt Lts</a> Julie Gibson and Sally Cox were the RAF's first female pilots to fly in an RAF jet aircraft – a <a href="/wiki/BAC_Jet_Provost" title="BAC Jet Provost">BAC Jet Provost</a> – with <a href="/wiki/No._1_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 1 Flying Training School RAF">1 FTS</a>. Sally Cox was later the first female RAF pilot selected for fast jet training at <a href="/wiki/Royal_Marines_Base_Chivenor" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Marines Base Chivenor">RAF Chivenor</a> on 14 January 1992 on the No.128 Course with the Hawk, but she withdrew seven weeks into the 17-week tactical weapons courses (No.2 Tactical Weapons Unit) in March 1992 as she found low flying too stressful. Julie would later be the RAF's first female <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_C-130_Hercules" title="Lockheed C-130 Hercules">Hercules</a> captain in 1998; she was the first female pilot to qualify at <a href="/wiki/No._6_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 6 Flying Training School RAF">6 FTS</a> at RAF Finningley. On 1 March 1991, the RAF's first female navigator, <a href="/wiki/Flying_officer" title="Flying officer">Flying Officer</a> <a href="/wiki/Anne-Marie_Dawe" title="Anne-Marie Dawe">Anne-Marie Dawe</a>, began with 6 FTS at RAF Finningley; she later flew on the Hercules at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Lyneham" title="RAF Lyneham">RAF Lyneham</a>. On 20 July 1989 it had been announced that female RAF pilots could fly in non-combat roles.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_history">Industrial history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Industrial history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_steel_industry_(1850%E2%80%931970)" title="History of the steel industry (1850–1970)">History of the steel industry (1850–1970)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_Brearley_from_Sheffield.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Harry_Brearley_from_Sheffield.jpg/220px-Harry_Brearley_from_Sheffield.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Harry_Brearley_from_Sheffield.jpg/330px-Harry_Brearley_from_Sheffield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/Harry_Brearley_from_Sheffield.jpg 2x" data-file-width="361" data-file-height="430" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_Brearley_from_Sheffield.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Harry Brearley, inventor of stainless steel; in 1821, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Berthier" title="Pierre Berthier">Pierre Berthier</a>, a French mining engineer, had also found that adding <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a> to steel made it more resistant to chemical attack</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">Stainless steel</a> was invented in 1913 by <a href="/wiki/Harry_Brearley" title="Harry Brearley">Harry Brearley</a> of Sheffield, working for <a href="/wiki/Firth_Brown_Steels" title="Firth Brown Steels">Firth Brown Steels</a> (now called <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Forgemasters" title="Sheffield Forgemasters">Sheffield Forgemasters</a>). <a href="/wiki/SAE_304_stainless_steel" title="SAE 304 stainless steel">SAE 304 stainless steel</a> is the most common stainless steel today, invented by Dr <a href="/wiki/William_Herbert_Hatfield" title="William Herbert Hatfield">William Herbert Hatfield</a> FRS in 1924 at Firth-Vickers known as Staybrite 18/8, a chromium-nickel steel. Many Swiss watches were mostly made out of Staybrite.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Crucible_steel" title="Crucible steel">Crucible steel</a> had been invented in the 1740s by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Huntsman" title="Benjamin Huntsman">Benjamin Huntsman</a> of <a href="/wiki/Epworth,_Lincolnshire" title="Epworth, Lincolnshire">Epworth</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">Lincolnshire</a> (part of the region); previously steel was made by the <a href="/wiki/Cementation_process" title="Cementation process">cementation process</a>; the 1856 <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_process" title="Bessemer process">Bessemer process</a> allowed much more steel to be made. Sheffield early steel used Swedish <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">bar iron</a> as it had less <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>. The fast acting tilting <a href="/wiki/Converting_(metallurgy)" title="Converting (metallurgy)">converter</a> was a huge improvement in 1860 for Sheffield steel; the process meant high-phosphorus <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a> from local mines could be used. At one point, Sheffield was producing 10,000&#160;tons of steel a week. The <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgist</a> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eric_Colbeck&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Eric Colbeck (page does not exist)">Eric Colbeck</a>, when working at <a href="/wiki/Hadfields_Limited" title="Hadfields Limited">Hadfields Limited</a> in Sheffield, invented <a href="/wiki/Alloy_steel" title="Alloy steel">boron steel</a> for <a href="/wiki/Control_rod" title="Control rod">control rods</a> for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>. Metallurgists at Firth-Vickers developed the heat-resistant stainless steel (Rex 78) for the <a href="/wiki/Turbine_blade" title="Turbine blade">turbine blades</a> of <a href="/wiki/Frank_Whittle" title="Frank Whittle">Frank Whittle</a>'s first engines, and Britain's first jet aircraft. <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall_(industrialist)" title="John Marshall (industrialist)">John Marshall</a> of Yorkshire developed the first-ever iron-framed buildings (industrial mills). Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hadfield" title="Robert Hadfield">Robert Hadfield</a> <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a> invented <a href="/wiki/Mangalloy" title="Mangalloy">manganese steel</a> in 1882, and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_steel" title="Electrical steel">silicon steel</a>, which allowed the development of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">electromagnets</a> and consequently electricity; the Hadfields site is now <a href="/wiki/Meadowhall_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Meadowhall Centre">Meadowhall Centre</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Matthew_Murray" title="Matthew Murray">Matthew Murray</a> of Leeds built the first commercially successful <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotive</a> (<a href="/wiki/Salamanca_(locomotive)" title="Salamanca (locomotive)"><i>Salamanca</i></a>) in 1812. The <a href="/wiki/Doncaster_Works" title="Doncaster Works">Doncaster Works</a> at <a href="/wiki/Hexthorpe" title="Hexthorpe">Hexthorpe</a> built Britain's most famous steam trains, for the <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_Railway_(Great_Britain)" title="Great Northern Railway (Great Britain)">GNR</a>, then <a href="/wiki/London_and_North_Eastern_Railway" title="London and North Eastern Railway">LNER</a> (from 1923). The <a href="/wiki/Manchester%E2%80%93Sheffield%E2%80%93Wath_electric_railway" title="Manchester–Sheffield–Wath electric railway">Manchester–Sheffield–Wath electric railway</a> was the UK's longest electrified railway in 1953 when opened. What is now West Yorkshire in the nineteenth century was the heart of <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacture_during_the_Industrial_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Textile manufacture during the Industrial Revolution">Britain's woollen weaving wills</a>, often using <a href="/wiki/Alpaca_fiber" title="Alpaca fiber">Alpaca fiber</a> and by connections to the important <a href="/wiki/Leeds_and_Liverpool_Canal" title="Leeds and Liverpool Canal">Leeds and Liverpool Canal</a>. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bramah" title="Joseph Bramah">Joseph Bramah</a> of Barnsley was important in the development of <a href="/wiki/Hydraulics" title="Hydraulics">hydraulics</a>, and also developed the <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_press" title="Hydraulic press">hydraulic press</a> in 1795 and in 1778 the <a href="/wiki/Flush_toilet" title="Flush toilet">flush toilet</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:What_was_Coal_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_868804.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/What_was_Coal_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_868804.jpg/170px-What_was_Coal_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_868804.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/What_was_Coal_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_868804.jpg/255px-What_was_Coal_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_868804.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/What_was_Coal_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_868804.jpg/340px-What_was_Coal_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_868804.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:What_was_Coal_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_868804.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Former Coal House in Doncaster; the peak production of <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Coal mining in the United Kingdom">coal in the UK</a> was in 1913, when 9% of the country's male labour force worked down the pit; in the 1970s Britain consumed 2m tonnes of coal a week, which was strategically reduced by reliance on <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea gas</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Aspdin" title="Joseph Aspdin">Joseph Aspdin</a> from Leeds invented <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a> in 1824; when mixed with <a href="/wiki/Construction_aggregate" title="Construction aggregate">aggregates</a> and sand, this produced concrete. <a href="/wiki/John_Smeaton" title="John Smeaton">John Smeaton</a> was a famous civil engineer from Leeds, and notable for building the first modern-day lighthouse (<a href="/wiki/Eddystone_Lighthouse" title="Eddystone Lighthouse">Eddystone Lighthouse</a>) and for developing <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_lime" title="Hydraulic lime">hydraulic lime</a> (it is able to set under water), which would lead to <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">cement</a>. Sir <a href="/wiki/Donald_Bailey_(civil_engineer)" title="Donald Bailey (civil engineer)">Donald Bailey (civil engineer)</a>, of Rotherham, invented the <a href="/wiki/Bailey_bridge" title="Bailey bridge">Bailey bridge</a> which was important in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Allied_invasion_of_Germany" title="Western Allied invasion of Germany">later stages</a> of the Second World War for crossing rivers; the design took place in 1940 at the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Experimental_Bridging_Establishment&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Experimental Bridging Establishment (page does not exist)">Experimental Bridging Establishment</a> in <a href="/wiki/Christchurch,_Dorset" title="Christchurch, Dorset">Christchurch, Dorset</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hampshire" title="Hampshire">Hampshire</a> before 1974).
</p><p>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarence_Flour_Mill&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Clarence Flour Mill (page does not exist)">Clarence Flour Mill</a> was built in 1891 in Hull and produced Britain's first <a href="/wiki/White_bread" title="White bread">white bread</a>; it became part of <a href="/wiki/Rank_Hovis_McDougall" title="Rank Hovis McDougall">Rank Hovis McDougall</a> (RHM) in 1962. Smith &amp; Nephew launched <a href="/wiki/Elastoplast" title="Elastoplast">Elastoplast</a> in 1928; since 1992 this has been owned by the German <a href="/wiki/Beiersdorf" title="Beiersdorf">Beiersdorf</a> company. <a href="/wiki/James_Henry_Atkinson" title="James Henry Atkinson">James Henry Atkinson</a> of Leeds invented the <a href="/wiki/Mousetrap" title="Mousetrap">mousetrap</a> in 1898. <a href="/w/index.php?title=ICI_Fibres&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="ICI Fibres (page does not exist)">ICI Fibres</a> was headquartered in the <a href="/wiki/Crimple_Valley" title="Crimple Valley">Crimple Valley</a> south of Harrogate; the site is now known as <a href="/wiki/Hornbeam_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Hornbeam Park">Hornbeam Park</a>. The <a href="/wiki/National_Coal_Board" title="National Coal Board">National Coal Board</a> (NCB) was based at <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coal_House,_Doncaster&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Coal House, Doncaster (page does not exist)">Coal House, Doncaster</a> on the A18. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Spencer_(Marks_and_Spencer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Spencer (Marks and Spencer)">Thomas Spencer</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Marks_%26_Spencer" title="Marks &amp; Spencer">M&amp;S</a>, was born in Skipton in 1851. <a href="/wiki/Topshop" title="Topshop">Topshop</a> was founded in 1964 in Sheffield, part of a <a href="/wiki/Peter_Robinson_(department_store)" title="Peter Robinson (department store)">Peter Robinson</a> shop, itself owned by <a href="/wiki/Burton_(retailer)" title="Burton (retailer)">Burton</a>; Topshop became an independent chain in 1973.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:York%27s_Chocolate_Story_(8431).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/York%27s_Chocolate_Story_%288431%29.jpg/220px-York%27s_Chocolate_Story_%288431%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/York%27s_Chocolate_Story_%288431%29.jpg/330px-York%27s_Chocolate_Story_%288431%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/York%27s_Chocolate_Story_%288431%29.jpg/440px-York%27s_Chocolate_Story_%288431%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:York%27s_Chocolate_Story_(8431).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>York's Chocolate Story in central York; on 28 April 1942 in the intense <a href="/wiki/Baedeker_Blitz" title="Baedeker Blitz">Baedeker raid</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rowntree%27s" title="Rowntree&#39;s">Rowntree's</a> factory in North Street received a direct hit and all of its sugar caught fire</div></div></div>
<p>From 1945 to 2002 all the <a href="/wiki/Tennis_ball" title="Tennis ball">tennis balls</a> for <a href="/wiki/The_Championships,_Wimbledon" title="The Championships, Wimbledon">Wimbledon</a> were made by <a href="/wiki/Slazenger" title="Slazenger">Slazenger</a> off the A61 in the south of Barnsley. Since 2006, <a href="/wiki/Track_(rail_transport)" title="Track (rail transport)">tracks</a> for the UK's railways have been made at <a href="/wiki/Scunthorpe_Steelworks" title="Scunthorpe Steelworks">Scunthorpe</a>, and much is made for Europe's railways (mainly France, although rolled at <a href="/wiki/Hayange" title="Hayange">Hayange</a>). <i><a href="/wiki/Terry%27s_Chocolate_Orange" title="Terry&#39;s Chocolate Orange">Terry's Chocolate Orange</a></i>, first made in 1932, are now made since 2005 by Kraft Foods Polska at <a href="/w/index.php?title=Fabryka_w_Jankowicach&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Fabryka w Jankowicach (page does not exist)">Fabryka w Jankowicach</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jankowice,_Greater_Poland_Voivodeship" title="Jankowice, Greater Poland Voivodeship">Jankowice</a>, having been bought by <a href="/wiki/Kraft_Foods" title="Kraft Foods">Kraft</a> in 1993; <i><a href="/wiki/Smarties" title="Smarties">Smarties</a></i>, first made in York in 1937, since 2006 are made at <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chocoladen-Werk_Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Chocoladen-Werk Hamburg (page does not exist)">Chocoladen-Werk Hamburg</a>, with the brand being owned by <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9" title="Nestlé">Nestlé</a> since 1988; the <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9_Deutschland" title="Nestlé Deutschland">Nestlé Deutschland</a> site also makes <i>Kit Kat</i>, first made in 1935 and named in 1937, and <i>After Eight</i>, first made in 1962. Mackintosh's of Halifax invented <a href="/wiki/Quality_Street_(confectionery)" title="Quality Street (confectionery)">Quality Street</a> in 1936, <a href="/wiki/Rolo" title="Rolo">Rolo</a> in 1937, <a href="/wiki/Munchies_(confectionery)" title="Munchies (confectionery)">Munchies</a> in 1957, <a href="/wiki/Caramac" title="Caramac">Caramac</a> in 1959, and <a href="/wiki/Toffee_Crisp" class="mw-redirect" title="Toffee Crisp">Toffee Crisp</a> in 1963. Rowntree's invented  <a href="/wiki/Rowntree%27s_Fruit_Gums" title="Rowntree&#39;s Fruit Gums">Fruit Gums</a> in 1893, <a href="/wiki/Aero_(chocolate)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aero (chocolate)">Aero</a> in 1935, being still made in York on <a href="/wiki/Haxby" title="Haxby">Haxby</a> Road, and <a href="/wiki/Polo_(confectionery)" title="Polo (confectionery)">Polos</a> in 1948 (although actually developed before WWII).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Central_Electricity_Generating_Board" title="Central Electricity Generating Board">CEGB</a>'s 400&#160;kV <a href="/wiki/National_Grid_(Great_Britain)" title="National Grid (Great Britain)">Supergrid</a> was first tested on 15 December 1963 when <a href="/wiki/High_Marnham_Power_Station" title="High Marnham Power Station">High Marnham Power Station</a> connected on a 64-mile (previous 275kV) line to <a href="/wiki/Monk_Fryston" title="Monk Fryston">Monk Fryston</a> substation (near the Selby Fork junction); the 420-ton transformers at either end were from <a href="/wiki/English_Electric" title="English Electric">English Electric</a> at <a href="/wiki/Stafford" title="Stafford">Stafford</a>. The first section of the (first) 275kV supergrid opened in 1951 from <a href="/wiki/Staythorpe_Power_Station" title="Staythorpe Power Station">Staythorpe Power Station</a> (Nottinghamshire) to <a href="/wiki/West_Melton" title="West Melton">West Melton</a> (<a href="/wiki/Brampton_Bierlow" title="Brampton Bierlow">Brampton Bierlow</a>), west of Wath-upon-Dearne. Much of West Yorkshire's connection to the southern section of the National Grid (along the Trent Valley, also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Megawatt_Valley" title="Megawatt Valley">Megawatt Valley</a></i>) is either through High Marnham (to <a href="/wiki/Thurcroft" title="Thurcroft">Thurcroft</a> and on to Sheffield and South Yorkshire) or Creyke Beck (north of Cottingham in <a href="/wiki/Skidby" title="Skidby">Skidby</a>) to the east, (with connection via Thornton near Pocklington); <a href="/wiki/Thornton,_East_Riding_of_Yorkshire" title="Thornton, East Riding of Yorkshire">Thornton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osbaldwick" title="Osbaldwick">Osbaldwick</a> (next to the A1079 in the east of York) are the only connections from Yorkshire to the north, connecting to <a href="/wiki/Norton,_County_Durham" title="Norton, County Durham">Norton</a> (near <a href="/wiki/Carlton,_County_Durham" title="Carlton, County Durham">Carlton</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Newby,_Hambleton" title="Newby, Hambleton">Newby</a> near <a href="/wiki/Teesside" title="Teesside">Teesside</a>; Bradford West, apart from Monk Fryston, is the other main substation for the area
</p><p>FLUENT (owned since 2006 by <a href="/wiki/Ansys" title="Ansys">Ansys</a>) was developed at the University of Sheffield, and became the world's leading software for <a href="/wiki/Computational_fluid_dynamics" title="Computational fluid dynamics">CFD</a>, launched in October 1983. <a href="/wiki/Helen_Sharman" title="Helen Sharman">Helen Sharman</a> OBE, from Sheffield and a former <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sheffield" title="University of Sheffield">University of Sheffield</a>-educated chemist who developed chocolate for <a href="/wiki/Mars,_Incorporated" title="Mars, Incorporated">Mars</a>, was the first Briton in space on the <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_TM-12" title="Soyuz TM-12">Soyuz TM-12</a> mission on 18 May 1991, aged 27 (she is still the fifth-youngest person to have flown into space), landing with <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_TM-11" title="Soyuz TM-11">Soyuz TM-11</a> eight days later.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/David_Mellor_(designer)" title="David Mellor (designer)">David Mellor</a> of Sheffield produced the standard design for <a href="/wiki/Street_furniture" title="Street furniture">street furniture</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Traffic_light" title="Traffic light">traffic lights</a>, in the UK from 1973. The US had installed its first electric traffic lights in August 1914 in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, and traffic lights were first introduced permanently in the UK on 16 March 1928 in Leeds at the junction of <a href="/wiki/Park_Row,_Leeds" title="Park Row, Leeds">Park Row, Leeds</a> and Bond Street, although were not vehicle-actuated; there had been a one-day trial of traffic lights in Wolverhampton on 11 February 1928.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_heritage">Scientific heritage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Scientific heritage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Harrison" title="John Harrison">John Harrison</a> of <a href="/wiki/Barrow_upon_Humber" title="Barrow upon Humber">Barrow upon Humber</a> (but born in Yorkshire at <a href="/wiki/Foulby" title="Foulby">Foulby</a>), invented the <a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">marine chronometer</a> which allowed <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> to be accurately measured, and hence navigation at sea. This allowed Captain <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> of Whitby to colonise much of Australia. Harrison also invented the <a href="/wiki/Bimetallic_strip" title="Bimetallic strip">bimetallic strip</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rolling-element_bearing" title="Rolling-element bearing">rolling bearing</a>, without which most vehicles could not move.
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_photography_technology" title="Timeline of photography technology">Timeline of photography technology</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Albert_Crewe" title="Albert Crewe">Albert Crewe</a>, born near Huddersfield in 1927 and attended <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar school</a> in Bradford, invented the <a href="/wiki/Scanning_transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Scanning transmission electron microscopy">scanning electron microscope</a> in 1970 at the <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a> (US). <a href="/wiki/John_Carbutt" title="John Carbutt">John Carbutt</a> (1832–1905), from Sheffield and moved to America, founded his <a href="/w/index.php?title=Keystone_Dry_Plate_Works&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Keystone Dry Plate Works (page does not exist)">Keystone Dry Plate Works</a> in 1879 and was the first to develop sheets of <a href="/wiki/Celluloid" title="Celluloid">celluloid</a> coated with <a href="/wiki/Photographic_emulsion" title="Photographic emulsion">photographic emulsion</a> for making films in 1888, and for <a href="/wiki/William_Kennedy_Dickson" title="William Kennedy Dickson">William Kennedy Dickson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Kinetoscope" title="Kinetoscope">Kinetoscope</a> around 1890, which set the <a href="/wiki/35mm_movie_film" class="mw-redirect" title="35mm movie film">35mm</a> standard for <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">motion</a> and still cameras. <a href="/wiki/Roundhay_Garden_Scene" title="Roundhay Garden Scene">Roundhay Garden Scene</a>, shot on 14 October 1888 by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Le_Prince" title="Louis Le Prince">Louis Le Prince</a>, is the oldest surviving film in existence; <a href="/wiki/Sound_film" title="Sound film">'talking' movies</a> appeared after 1927. <a href="/wiki/Harold_Dennis_Taylor" title="Harold Dennis Taylor">Dennis Taylor</a> of <a href="/wiki/T._Cooke_%26_Sons" title="T. Cooke &amp; Sons">T. Cooke &amp; Sons</a> of York invented the <a href="/wiki/Cooke_triplet" title="Cooke triplet">Cooke triplet</a> in 1893 to stop <a href="/wiki/Optical_aberration" title="Optical aberration">optical aberration</a>, and allow fine definition up to the outer edge of lenses. Prof Sir <a href="/wiki/George_William_Gray" title="George William Gray">George William Gray</a> developed <a href="/wiki/Liquid-crystal_display" title="Liquid-crystal display">LCD displays</a> at Hull, when working for the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Defence (United Kingdom)">MoD</a> (<a href="/wiki/Royal_Radar_Establishment" title="Royal Radar Establishment">RRE</a>) in Malvern, developing it with the substance <a href="/wiki/4-Cyano-4%27-pentylbiphenyl" title="4-Cyano-4&#39;-pentylbiphenyl">cyano-biphenyl</a> in 1973; the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_fluorescent_display" title="Vacuum fluorescent display">VFD</a> (a similar purpose) was invented in Japan in 1967.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Smithson_Tennant" title="Smithson Tennant">Smithson Tennant</a> born Selby, educated in Beverley, discovered <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a> in 1803; the two are atomic numbers 76 and 77, with osmium being the densest naturally occurring element, and iridium is the next densest. The worldwide deposition of <a href="/wiki/Iridium_anomaly" title="Iridium anomaly">high concentrations of iridium</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_boundary" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary">geological K-T</a> <a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy" title="Stratigraphy">stratigraphic boundary</a>, and that locally of <a href="/wiki/Shocked_quartz" title="Shocked quartz">shocked quartz</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a>, led to a theory by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Alvarez" title="Walter Alvarez">Walter Alvarez</a> in 1980 of an <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">asteroid impact</a> at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period, known as the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>, that <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">wiped out</a> the dinosaurs. <a href="/wiki/Fred_Hoyle" title="Fred Hoyle">Fred Hoyle</a> was an important physicist from <a href="/wiki/Bingley" title="Bingley">Bingley</a>, who discovered how stars <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">made chemical elements</a>, and originated the phrase <i><a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">big bang</a></i> in 1949. An expanding universe (and consequent Big Bang) was first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lemaître">Georges Lemaître</a> of Belgium to overcome <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>'s 1917 <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Static_universe" title="Static universe">static universe</a>, and was only widely accepted after the 1930s. The clergyman <a href="/wiki/John_Michell" title="John Michell">John Michell</a>, a vicar of <a href="/wiki/Thornhill,_West_Yorkshire" title="Thornhill, West Yorkshire">Thornhill</a>, was the first person to calculate a realistic distance of stars, observed in the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a>, from Earth. As early as the 18th century he was also the first to propose that light from the stars could be deflected by their own gravity, and has been described as the father of <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a> and magnetometry.
</p><p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenneth_Bentley&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kenneth Bentley (page does not exist)">Kenneth Bentley</a> of Reckitt &amp; Colman at Hull in 1963 discovered his <a href="/wiki/Bentley_compounds" title="Bentley compounds">Bentley compounds</a> (orvinols), which included
<a href="/wiki/Etorphine" title="Etorphine">etorphine</a>, which for many years was the most-powerful <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">analgesic</a>, and is the drug most often used to safely immobilise elephants and other large animals, and is now manufactured solely by <a href="/wiki/MacFarlan_Smith" title="MacFarlan Smith">MacFarlan Smith</a> of Edinburgh. Prof <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lowry_Turner" title="Robert Lowry Turner">Robert Lowry Turner</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Whyte-Watson" title="George Whyte-Watson">George Whyte-Watson</a> developed <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer_chemotherapy" title="Breast cancer chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a> for <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a> in 1959 at <a href="/wiki/Bradford_Royal_Infirmary" title="Bradford Royal Infirmary">Bradford Royal Infirmary</a>.
</p><p>At the former <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Royal_Infirmary" title="Sheffield Royal Infirmary">Sheffield Royal Infirmary</a> in the north-west of the city centre on 25 November 1930, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cecil_Paine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cecil Paine (page does not exist)">Cecil Paine</a>, a pathologist, cured patients with <a href="/wiki/Eye_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Eye disease">eye infections</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Streptococcus_pneumoniae" title="Streptococcus pneumoniae">pneumococcus</a>)
completely with Penicillin, having been a <a href="/wiki/Bacteriology" title="Bacteriology">bacteriology</a> student of Alexander Fleming two years previously, and had grown <i>Penicillium notatum</i> (now called <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium_chrysogenum" title="Penicillium chrysogenum">Penicillium chrysogenum</a></i>) in some meat <a href="/wiki/Broth" title="Broth">broth</a>. This was the first-ever cure of human <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a> with an <a href="/wiki/Antibiotics" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibiotics">antibiotic</a>, and only gained public attention in 1983 when his medical notes were emerged by Prof <a href="/wiki/Milton_Wainwright" title="Milton Wainwright">Milton Wainwright</a> in an office in <a href="/wiki/Firth_Court" title="Firth Court">Firth Court</a>. <a href="/wiki/Howard_Florey" title="Howard Florey">Howard Florey</a> was working as a professor of pathology from 1931 to 1935 at the city's university, and Paine's work gave impetus for Florey to work out how to purify <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a>, but it would be February 1941 before he treated his first patient. <a href="/wiki/Prontosil" title="Prontosil">Prontosil</a>, discovered by <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Domagk" title="Gerhard Domagk">Gerhard Domagk</a> (receiving the 1939 Nobel Prize for Medicine) at <a href="/wiki/Bayer" title="Bayer">Bayer</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wuppertal" title="Wuppertal">Wuppertal</a>, Germany in 1932, would be the first commercially available (a <a href="/wiki/Sulfonamide_(medicine)" title="Sulfonamide (medicine)">sulfonamide</a>) antibiotic.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Venn" title="John Venn">John Venn</a>, born in Hull although moved to London, developed his well-known and ubiquitous <a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">diagrams</a> in 1880. <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kilburn" title="Tom Kilburn">Tom Kilburn</a> from <a href="/wiki/Dewsbury" title="Dewsbury">Dewsbury</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Calland_Williams" title="Frederic Calland Williams">Freddie Williams</a>, developed the <a href="/wiki/Williams_tube" title="Williams tube">Williams tube</a>, the first computer <a href="/wiki/Binary_data" title="Binary data">binary data</a> storage.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The RAF's first heavy bomber, the <a href="/wiki/Short_Stirling" title="Short Stirling">Short Stirling</a>, entered service at RAF Leeming with <a href="/wiki/No._7_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 7 Squadron RAF">7 Sqn</a>. On 2 November 1940, RAF Linton-on-Ouse received the first <a href="/wiki/Handley_Page_Halifax" title="Handley Page Halifax">Handley Page Halifax</a> with <a href="/wiki/No._35_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 35 Squadron RAF">35 Sqn</a>. <a href="/wiki/Avro" title="Avro">Avro</a> built around 4,500 <a href="/wiki/Avro_Anson" title="Avro Anson">Ansons</a> at its RAF Yeadon shadow factory, and around 700 <a href="/wiki/Avro_Lancaster" title="Avro Lancaster">Lancasters</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Dyke_Band_World_Band_Festival_Luzern_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Black_Dyke_Band_World_Band_Festival_Luzern_2012.jpg/220px-Black_Dyke_Band_World_Band_Festival_Luzern_2012.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Black_Dyke_Band_World_Band_Festival_Luzern_2012.jpg/330px-Black_Dyke_Band_World_Band_Festival_Luzern_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Black_Dyke_Band_World_Band_Festival_Luzern_2012.jpg/440px-Black_Dyke_Band_World_Band_Festival_Luzern_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Dyke_Band_World_Band_Festival_Luzern_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Black Dyke Band at the <a href="/wiki/Lucerne_Culture_and_Congress_Centre" title="Lucerne Culture and Congress Centre">Lucerne Culture and Congress Centre</a> (KKL Luzern) in 2012</div></div></div>
<p>The area is well known for its brass bands, with the <a href="/wiki/Brighouse_and_Rastrick_Brass_Band" title="Brighouse and Rastrick Brass Band">Brighouse and Rastrick Brass Band</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Dyke_Band" title="Black Dyke Band">Black Dyke Band</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grimethorpe_Colliery_Band" title="Grimethorpe Colliery Band">Grimethorpe Colliery Band</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlton_Main_Frickley_Colliery_Band" title="Carlton Main Frickley Colliery Band">Carlton Main Frickley Colliery Band</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rothwell_Temperance_Band" title="Rothwell Temperance Band">Rothwell Temperance Band</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hammonds_Saltaire_Band" title="Hammonds Saltaire Band">Hammonds Saltaire Band</a>. <a href="/wiki/Brass_band_(British_style)" class="mw-redirect" title="Brass band (British style)">Brass bands</a> have mostly been linked with coal mines. <a href="/wiki/John_Bacchus_Dykes" title="John Bacchus Dykes">John Bacchus Dykes</a> from Hull wrote the hymn tunes for <i><a href="/wiki/The_King_of_Love_My_Shepherd_Is" title="The King of Love My Shepherd Is">The King of Love My Shepherd Is</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Eternal_Father,_Strong_to_Save" title="Eternal Father, Strong to Save">Eternal Father, Strong to Save</a></i>, and arranged <i><a href="/wiki/We_Plough_the_Fields_and_Scatter" title="We Plough the Fields and Scatter">We Plough the Fields and Scatter</a></i>. The <i>Cranbrook</i> tune to <i><a href="/wiki/On_Ilkla_Moor_Baht_%27at" title="On Ilkla Moor Baht &#39;at">On Ilkla Moor Baht 'at</a></i> was written by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Clark_(composer)" title="Thomas Clark (composer)">Thomas Clark</a>, a Kent shoemaker.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bront%C3%AB_family" title="Brontë family">Brontë family</a> are the region's best known authors. The region's most popular city for tourists is York.
</p><p>Castleford is the birthplace of the world-famous sculptor Henry Moore. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Chippendale" title="Thomas Chippendale">Thomas Chippendale</a> was an important furniture maker, born in Otley in 1718. <i><a href="/wiki/Rita,_Sue_and_Bob_Too" title="Rita, Sue and Bob Too">Rita, Sue and Bob Too</a></i> is a classic 1987 film made around Bradford; other classic regional films include <a href="/wiki/Ken_Loach" title="Ken Loach">Ken Loach</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Kes_(film)" title="Kes (film)">Kes</a></i>, a 1969 film made in the Barnsley area, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Full_Monty" title="The Full Monty">The Full Monty</a></i> a 1997 film made around Sheffield, and <i><a href="/wiki/Brassed_Off" title="Brassed Off">Brassed Off</a></i>, a 1996 film made in the Doncaster and Barnsley area.
</p><p>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bradford_Small_Livestock_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bradford Small Livestock Society (page does not exist)">Bradford Small Livestock Society</a> has held the national championships for <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a> since 1921, held at the <a href="/wiki/Great_Yorkshire_Showground" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Yorkshire Showground">Great Yorkshire Showground</a> in Harrogate, and holds its <a href="/w/index.php?title=Small_Animal_Show&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Small Animal Show (page does not exist)">Small Animal Show</a> for other pets. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_Hinchliffe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Charles Hinchliffe (page does not exist)">Charles Hinchliffe</a> of Huddersfield pioneered the idea of the <a href="/wiki/Farm_shop" title="Farm shop">farm shop</a> in 1974. <a href="/wiki/Donald_Watson" title="Donald Watson">Donald Watson</a> from Mexborough invented the term <i><a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">vegan</a></i> in 1944. <a href="/wiki/Seebohm_Rowntree" title="Seebohm Rowntree">Seebohm Rowntree</a> was an important social researcher from York, and had a big impact on thinking on the subject.
</p><p>On 6 January (<a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Night_(holiday)" title="Twelfth Night (holiday)">Twelfth Night</a>) each year <a href="/wiki/Haxey" title="Haxey">Haxey</a> in North Lincolnshire holds its <a href="/wiki/Haxey_Hood" title="Haxey Hood">Haxey Hood</a>. On <a href="/wiki/Easter_Monday" title="Easter Monday">Easter Monday</a> each year <a href="/wiki/Gawthorpe,_Wakefield" title="Gawthorpe, Wakefield">Gawthorpe</a>, near Wakefield, holds the <a href="/w/index.php?title=World_Coal_Carrying_Championship&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="World Coal Carrying Championship (page does not exist)">World Coal Carrying Championship</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_assembly">Regional assembly</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Regional assembly">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg/220px-Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg/330px-Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg/440px-Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Cathedral" title="Wakefield Cathedral">Wakefield Cathedral</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_and_Humber_Assembly" title="Yorkshire and Humber Assembly">Yorkshire and Humber Assembly</a> was a partnership of all local authorities in the region and representatives of various economic, social and environmental sectors. The full Assembly normally met three times a year, normally in February, June and October.
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The full Assembly is responsible for providing regional leadership, agreeing regional strategic priorities, directing the development of the Integrated Regional Framework and endorsing key regional strategies. Membership comprises all 22 local authorities in this region, plus 15 Social, Economic and Environmental partners, and the National Parks for planning purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>On 31 March 2009, the Assembly was abolished and replaced by <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Yorkshire_and_Humber" title="Local Government Yorkshire and Humber">Local Government Yorkshire and Humber</a>, which continues to be based in the former Assembly premises in King Street in <a href="/wiki/Wakefield" title="Wakefield">Wakefield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Yorkshire is one of the two regions (along with the <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West</a>) that were expected to have a referendum about the establishment of an elected <a href="/wiki/Regional_Assemblies_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional Assemblies in England">regional assembly</a>. When the <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">North East</a> region of England rejected having an elected regional assembly in a referendum, the then <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Deputy Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Prescott" title="John Prescott">John Prescott</a>  announced that he would not move orders for <a href="/wiki/2004_Northern_England_devolution_referendums" class="mw-redirect" title="2004 Northern England devolution referendums">other referendums</a> before the relevant provisions expired in June 2005.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Parliament">European Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: European Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_and_the_Humber_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="Yorkshire and the Humber (European Parliament constituency)">Yorkshire and the Humber (European Parliament constituency)</a></div>
<p>The European constituency of <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_and_the_Humber_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="Yorkshire and the Humber (European Parliament constituency)">Yorkshire and the Humber</a> is coterminous with the English region. After the <a href="/wiki/2014_European_Parliament_election" title="2014 European Parliament election">European Parliament election</a> in May 2014, Yorkshire and the Humber is represented by three <a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UK Independence Party</a> two <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">MEPs</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_results_2014">Election results 2014</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Election results 2014">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Population.2C_density_and_settlements"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Population,_density_and_settlements">Population, density and settlements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Population, density and settlements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The region has fewer degree-educated adults than the England average and the UK's highest rate of cancer.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teenage_pregnancy">Teenage pregnancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Teenage pregnancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For top-tier authorities, Kingston upon Hull has the highest <a href="/wiki/Teenage_pregnancy_and_sexual_health_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Teenage pregnancy and sexual health in the United Kingdom">teenage pregnancy</a> rate, closely followed by <a href="/wiki/North_East_Lincolnshire" title="North East Lincolnshire">North East Lincolnshire</a>. For top-tier authorities, <a href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire" title="North Yorkshire">North Yorkshire</a> has the lowest teenage pregnancy rate. For council districts, <a href="/wiki/Ryedale" title="Ryedale">Ryedale</a> has the lowest rate, closely followed by <a href="/wiki/Craven" title="Craven">Craven</a>. Only North Yorkshire has council districts in the region.
</p><p>Rotherham had the UK's youngest grandmother – 26 years old. Her 12-year-old daughter gave birth on 26 August 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_deprivation">Social deprivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Social deprivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For <a href="/wiki/Multiple_deprivation_index" title="Multiple deprivation index">multiple deprivation</a> in England, measured by the <a href="/wiki/Indices_of_deprivation_2007" title="Indices of deprivation 2007">Indices of deprivation 2007</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> the most deprived council districts in the region are, in descending order – Kingston upon Hull (11th in England), Bradford (32nd), Doncaster (41st), Barnsley (43rd), North East Lincolnshire (49th), Sheffield (63rd), Wakefield (66th), Rotherham (68th), Kirklees (82nd), Leeds (85th), and Scarborough (97th). These areas are mostly represented by Labour MPs, with a few Conservative MPs representing parts of Leeds (with a Lib Dem MP) and North East Lincolnshire, and all of Scarborough. Apart from Scarborough, they are unitary authorities.
</p><p>The least deprived districts are, in descending order – Harrogate, Hambleton, Richmondshire, Craven, and Selby – all in North Yorkshire. Like all of North Yorkshire, they are represented by Conservative MPs. At county level, the least deprived areas are, in descending order – North Yorkshire, York and the East Riding of Yorkshire which all have roughly the same level of deprivation, and lower than the majority of England, including <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northamptonshire" title="Northamptonshire">Northamptonshire</a>.
</p><p>The region as a whole is one of the more deprived in England, measured by having far more <a href="/wiki/Super_Output_Areas" class="mw-redirect" title="Super Output Areas">Lower Layer Super Output Areas</a> (LSOAs) in the 20% most deprived districts than the 20% least deprived districts.
</p><p>Kingston upon Hull has the highest proportion of people not in education, employment or training <a href="/wiki/NEET" title="NEET">NEETs</a> in the region (and fairly high for the UK – 10.6%).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This is another demographic extreme it shares with <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Borough_of_Knowsley" title="Metropolitan Borough of Knowsley">Knowsley</a> in Merseyside.
</p><p>In March 2011 the region had the third highest overall <a href="/wiki/Jobseeker%27s_Allowance" title="Jobseeker&#39;s Allowance">unemployment claimant</a> count in England with 4.4%. For the region, Hull has the highest rate with 7.8% which is the highest for any English district; <a href="/wiki/North_East_Lincolnshire" title="North East Lincolnshire">North East Lincolnshire</a> is next with 6.4%, and Doncaster has 5.2%. <a href="/wiki/Richmondshire" title="Richmondshire">Richmondshire</a> has the lowest rate with 1.8% and Harrogate is next lowest with 1.9%.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections">Elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_(Yorkshire_and_the_Humber).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28Yorkshire_and_the_Humber%29.svg/220px-United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28Yorkshire_and_the_Humber%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28Yorkshire_and_the_Humber%29.svg/330px-United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28Yorkshire_and_the_Humber%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28Yorkshire_and_the_Humber%29.svg/440px-United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_%28Yorkshire_and_the_Humber%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1089" data-file-height="1055" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_General_Election_2017_Results_Map_(Yorkshire_and_the_Humber).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Election results in 2017</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2015 United Kingdom general election">2015 general election</a>, 39% of the region's electorate voted Labour, 33% Conservative, 16% UKIP, 7% Liberal Democrat and 4% Green. Labour had almost twice as many seats than the Conservatives with 33 Labour, 19 Conservative and 2 Liberal Democrat. There was a 2.5% swing from Conservative to Labour. However, although Labour has around 60% of the region's seats, the geographic spread is mostly Conservative, due to the Labour seats having a much smaller geographic area. Since the <a href="/wiki/2017_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2017 United Kingdom general election">2017 general election</a> when <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Hallam_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Sheffield Hallam (UK Parliament constituency)">Sheffield Hallam</a> incumbent <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a> (Liberal Democrats) was defeated by Labour candidate <a href="/wiki/Jared_O%27Mara" title="Jared O&#39;Mara">Jared O'Mara</a>, all of <a href="/wiki/South_Yorkshire" title="South Yorkshire">South Yorkshire</a> is now held by Labour, and Labour is concentrated in the other coalfield areas and Hull.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurostat_NUTS">Eurostat NUTS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yorkshire_and_the_Humber&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Eurostat NUTS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a> <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature_of_Territorial_Units_for_Statistics" title="Nomenclature of Territorial Units for Statistics">Nomenclature of Territorial Units for Statistics</a> (NUTS), Yorkshire and the Humber is a level-1 NUTS region, coded "UKE", which is subdivided as follows:
